      <a href="/user/show/214200-don" class="user" title="Don">Don</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/214200-don?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/214200-don?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/214200-don?shelf=to-read-again" class="actionLinkLite">to-read-again</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/214200-don?shelf=india" class="actionLinkLite">india</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/214200-don?shelf=cantfindthisbooknow" class="actionLinkLite">cantfindthisbooknow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3709135">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2759376272266422501">It's interesting that Roy said in an interview that she'd never read Rushdie when compared to him. In retrospect that makes sense. I'd been struggling with "The Moors Last Sigh" when a friend from India gave me this book. I didn't pick it up for a few months and then fell into it, doing little else for days while I read it. At first I found that hard to believe, because she plays with language in ways that I thought Rushdie did, but later I could see that the way he works language is radically d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2759376272266422501">It's interesting that Roy said in an interview that she'd never read Rushdie when compared to him. In retrospect that makes sense. I'd been struggling with "The Moors Last Sigh" when a friend from India gave me this book. I didn't pick it up for a few months and then fell into it, doing little else for days while I read it. At first I found that hard to believe, because she plays with language in ways that I thought Rushdie did, but later I could see that the way he works language is radically different than how she did -- and I think I read a critique somewhere offering evidence of that that I can't remember now.<br /><br />This was a gorgeous book. I wept for a long time after reading it. I find it interesting that Roy said she doubted she would write another novel. She's a fascinating person, dedicated to the sorts of thinking about the world that I only give lip service to. She's amazing. Still, I'd love a second book, but I don't think she will. She's moved on to something different.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_65817657">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65817657" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="left imgcol" title="K.D. Absolutely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449978413p2/2156200.jpg" alt="K.D. Absolutely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/65817657?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 24, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely" class="user" title="K.D. Absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Hardcover 9780679457312" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      Man Booker, 1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core" class="actionLinkLite">1001-core</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=pulitzer" class="actionLinkLite">pulitzer</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=india" class="actionLinkLite">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65817657">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3452506086016471797">This is about the sad, poignant breakdown of an illustrious family in the 60’s in a place called Kerala, a state in India. It revolves around a woman called <strong>Ammu</strong> who has twins called <strong>Estha</strong> and <strong>Rahel</strong> and a lover called <strong>Velutha</strong>. She and her twins reside in an old house with her mother, <strong>Mamachi</strong> and her cunning aunt, <strong>Kochama</strong>. Her twins are not Velutha’s but of <strong>Baba’s.</strong> However, Ammu has left Baba because he is an alcoholic and tries to prostitute Ammu to his employer. Her mother and aunt are not in f</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3452506086016471797">This is about the sad, poignant breakdown of an illustrious family in the 60’s in a place called Kerala, a state in India. It revolves around a woman called <strong>Ammu</strong> who has twins called <strong>Estha</strong> and <strong>Rahel</strong> and a lover called <strong>Velutha</strong>. She and her twins reside in an old house with her mother, <strong>Mamachi</strong> and her cunning aunt, <strong>Kochama</strong>. Her twins are not Velutha’s but of <strong>Baba’s.</strong> However, Ammu has left Baba because he is an alcoholic and tries to prostitute Ammu to his employer. Her mother and aunt are not in favor of her marrying her lover Velutha because he is an “untouchable.” <br /><br />This novel won the 1997 Booker Prize and it was Roy’s first novel and she wrote this for a period of 4 years when she was 32-36 years old. She never had another book published and she is now 51 years old. An architect by profession, she, however, wrote essays and articles about her advocacies: anti-globalization movement, anti-neoimperialism and anti-global policies of the United States. Suffice it to say, that she may have only one bestseller and critically-acclaimed novel the whole of her life (unless she writes another one) but she is definitely not a flash-in-a-pan as she is a woman of substance. But still, just like Harper Lee with her <em>To Kill a Mockingbird</em>, my suspicious mind still asks this question: did Roy actually write this alone or was there somebody who helped her? In <em>To Kill</em>, Lee’s friend <em>Capote</em> said to have “helped” in writing that novel. <br /><br />But this is none of my business. She said she wrote it and I liked it so who cares. Every new novelist needs all the help he or she can get.<br /><br />Reading this book reminded me of the following:<blockquote> <em>Fugitive Pieces</em> by Anne Michaels for the fragmented, episodic and shifting narration. I think the idea is to mimic human mind: when you remember a story that happened in your life, you don’t think of it from the beginning to the end. The human brain does not work that way. In fact, the way Roy presented the story is like an well-arranged song where each part has its own highlight that kept me hooked and glued until the last pages where each character seems to contribute to the rising crescendo: the betrayal of Velutha by Estha after the accidental drowning of <strong>Sophie Mol</strong> followed by Velutha’s death and peaking to its climax with the almost-obscene lovemaking of Ammu and Velutha (that gave me headache for lack of sleep when I woke up this morning).<br /><br /><em> Beloved</em> by Toni Morrison for the dark, poignant social or racial discrimination told beautifully through poetic and lyrical. The innocent playful words of the fraternal twins, Rahel and Estha are, for me, just a step further from that Morrison’s characters, Denver (the mother) , Sethe (the living daughter) and Beloved (the ghost daughter) singing together beautifully towards the end of this Morrison’s 1987 Pulitzer-winning novel. <br /><br /><em>Midnight’s Children</em> by Salman Rushie for the political tone of the novel particularly on Velutha’s involvement in communist movement and the historical bits and pieces of India incorporated in the story.</blockquote> This book really seems to me like a excellent concoction of imported excellent ingredients. However, as it tastes strangely nice, it is still worth reading. Just content yourself as you cannot go back for a refill. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3452506086016471797">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_168163510">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168163510" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/541416-teresa" class="left imgcol" title="Teresa"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450070527p2/541416.jpg" alt="Teresa" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/168163510?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/541416-teresa" class="user" title="Teresa">Teresa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Hardcover 9780679457312" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer168163510">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1408130360257253778"><strong>4 and 1/2 stars</strong><br /><br />I admire this book: its structure, descriptive prose and portrayal of the children. One of the 'Two Things' (that's an allusion to the book) I heard about the novel before reading it was how sad and depressing it is. It is, especially in that the adults fail the children so spectacularly and, for the most part, intentionally (plus it's always hard to read of children as victims) but perhaps I've read so much sad, depressing fiction in my life that this one didn't stand out as more</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText1408130360257253778"><strong>4 and 1/2 stars</strong><br /><br />I admire this book: its structure, descriptive prose and portrayal of the children. One of the 'Two Things' (that's an allusion to the book) I heard about the novel before reading it was how sad and depressing it is. It is, especially in that the adults fail the children so spectacularly and, for the most part, intentionally (plus it's always hard to read of children as victims) but perhaps I've read so much sad, depressing fiction in my life that this one didn't stand out as more so. Or maybe it's because of the structure that states from the beginning that certain things have happened but leads you to the descriptions of these events slowly, as if it's bracing you for the intensity as you approach the 'Heart of Darkness.' Any time I wondered if the author was ever going to get to the alluded event, she did, and at the perfect time. Her wordplay (the other of the 'Two Things' I heard about the book beforehand) greatly alleviates the tragic elements. While we already know what ultimately happens to the couple at the end, I feel that the book ends on a positive note, as if these 'small things' are what need to be cherished.<br /><br />An image of Velutha in Rahel's mind from the first chapter stayed with me throughout and had me paging back several times to reread it. A simple mother-son allusion near the end to something in the beginning was perhaps more heartbreaking to me than anything else. The different viewpoints are done well, and seamlessly.<br /><br />I know a bit about the history of India, but I wish I knew more, as I believe this book can be read on more than one level. The pivotal year in the novel is 1969, which is when the Indian National Congress split into two factions (like two-egg twins?). Times are changing (with Marxism and Communism making some inroads) but not enough, as the government and religious and political organizations continue to fail individuals, the 'small things', paralleling the adults' failing the children.<br /><br />As I finished the book, I was reminded of a conversation I had the other day with a GR friend, in which I quoted Richard Ford. Ford was talking of racism in Mississippi, but it could apply to the caste system in India as well. He said, "[Mississippi] has so many writers, because it has so much to explain." At the same talk, he also said this of Faulkner's work: "[It] burns with the absurdity and incongruity of racism." I think Roy's novel also burns with this.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="1408130360257253778">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_930145863">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/930145863" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/14649015-poet-gentleness" class="left imgcol" title="Poet Gentleness"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1435386762p2/14649015.jpg" alt="Poet Gentleness" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/930145863?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 28, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/14649015-poet-gentleness" class="user" title="Poet Gentleness">Poet Gentleness</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Hardcover 9780679457312" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those who love without reservations</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Poet Gentleness by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5300620-steve-sckenda">Steve Sckenda</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/14649015-poet-gentleness?shelf=beauties" class="actionLinkLite">beauties</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14649015-poet-gentleness?shelf=political" class="actionLinkLite">political</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14649015-poet-gentleness?shelf=indian" class="actionLinkLite">indian</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14649015-poet-gentleness?shelf=to-be-re-read" class="actionLinkLite">to-be-re-read</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/14649015-poet-gentleness?shelf=books-that-make-u-think" class="actionLinkLite">books-that-make-u-think</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer930145863">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11939103164974964394"><strong> <blockquote> <em>Everything</em> out of the ordinary.<blockquote></blockquote> </blockquote> </strong><br /><br />It's ten-to-two.<br />It's ten-to-two on Rahel's painted watch.<br />It’s ten-to-two on Rahel’s painted watch which lies under the revolved earth of The History House in the Heart of the Darkness.<br /><br />It’ll be always ten-to-two on the stillness of Roy’s book as the derailed freight train of her story slams into our hearts. <br />It’ll be always ten-to-two when Sorrow, Pain, Unrequited Love, Too Much Love, and Unbearable, yet Understandable, Truths of Life collapse from their wagons a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11939103164974964394"><strong>
  <blockquote>
<em>Everything</em> out of the ordinary.<blockquote></blockquote>
</blockquote>
</strong><br /><br />It's ten-to-two.<br />It's ten-to-two on Rahel's painted watch.<br />It’s ten-to-two on Rahel’s painted watch which lies under the revolved earth of The History House in the Heart of the Darkness.<br /><br />It’ll be always ten-to-two on the stillness of Roy’s book as the derailed freight train of her story slams into our hearts. <br />It’ll be always ten-to-two when Sorrow, Pain, Unrequited Love, Too Much Love, and Unbearable, yet Understandable, Truths of Life collapse from their wagons and bury us all under them; <br />It’ll be always ten-to-two as the train’s sharp wheels scar our souls as deep as the ugly scars on Mammachi’s head, her blind soul carefully hidden by the gray hair and they will be there forever, for us to carry. <br />Ours will be beautiful scars.<br /><br />Scars… Healed scars. Scars healed by Unbearable Forbidden Necessary Cleaning <strong>Love</strong>, which will always be able to follow the Music escaping from a tangerine radio as it floats in the Air. <br /><br />The Still Air of Life.<br /><br />The Air of Roy’s story is filled with the haunting Truths of Life, so heavy to carry, they need to be shared, breathed by the twins, Esthappen, the boy-man, and Rahel, the girl-woman, as One. They are so horrible to be spoken of, that Rahel’s eyes becomes empty, empty <em>with</em> everything and Estha stops speaking, speaking <em>with</em> all. Inside. <br />But the Truths of Life leak as Mammachi’s Pickles’ bottles have leaked, impossible to be tamed into perfection, silent as a mute shriek of grief, imperceptible as a light cutting deep into darkness.<br /><br />As History evolves and revolves as the round World we live in, the skyblue old Plymouth, with its painted rack falling apart, thunders the careening story of Life and Death.<br /><br />Life and Death. Love and Hate. Angels and Demons. Humans and Beasts. Happiness and Rules. The Big Things and The Small Things, which in a reversal of their inherent nature belonged to the Small light God, who sweeps clean his steps as he walks backward, and the Big powerful God (god?), who stomps into the House with his dirty, muddied boots.<br /><br />Roy leads us past glass of pickles and jellies of Paradise Pickles &amp; Preserves, the factory; past <em>The Sound of Music</em>, the film; and past childhood, marriage, madness, pedophilia, poverty, violence, injustice and betrayal. And love, so much love.<br /><br />With no mercy, she tows us past the lost, hidden beauties and still there horrors of India; past confused Indians, immersed in caste hierarchy and lost in the war between British Imperialism and Karl Marx Communism; forced Evangelism; past Elvis Presley, Oxford, Coca-Cola, American TV shows and London life; all preferred, favorites in spite of the unique, laid-to-waste-in-twenty-minutes Kathakali dance. <br /><br />And she dresses us in saris of intolerance sewed carefully by single, married and widowed women and she gives us the painted masks of their unavailable, chauvinist kinsmen. <br /><br />For us, she disrobes the once-one turned-lonely children and two couples of forbidden lovers - who had already been bared, robbed… Loved less… The four of them The <em>Gods</em> of Small Things.<br /><br />And she makes us watch the Terror and the Love.<br /><br />I read this in two seatings only because I <em>had</em> to get a couple of hours’ sleep. I was frozen in my armchair, fossilized in time by the unjustified justice of my few smiles and many tears; nerves uncapped, shaking, almost hiding, as I saw many of my thoughts being SHOUTED OUT LOUD at me, <strong><em>from</em> me.</strong><br /><br />Will I read it again? Yes. Later. <em>(Lay. Ter.)</em><br /><br />Now, I need a moment. Of quiet emptiness.<br />To rage.<br /><br /><em>Et tu,</em> English, Indians, Christians, Syrian Christians, Hindus, Pelaya, Pulaya, Paravan, Touchables and Untouchables, Lower Middle Upper Classes, No Classes, all-and-yet-never Comrades! Who saw and looked away!<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[<em>Et tu,</em> Sophie Mol! The unfortunate English child <em>killed-killer</em> of the simple happiness of Rahel's and Estha’s childhood, the two-egg twin that was only One.<br /><br /><em>Et tu,</em> Pappachi, the Imperial Entomologist, domestic abuser, proud and full of cruel, ugly moths; Mammachi, the almost-blind beaten-wife and <em>example</em> of Christian beatitude; Vellya Paapen, the one with a mortgaged glass eye and the real blind one; Baby grand aunt Kochamma, the gullible girl turned bitter-sour, with her perfect Per-<strong>Nun</strong>-Ciation and unfair, hasty judgements and psychologic torture! Who played <em>alone-along</em> their parts, ignorantly not knowing life was no rehearsal!<br /><br /><em>Et tu,</em> poor Rahel and Estha! Children so loved less, from the Beginning until the End, the only one, forever un-living-dead bearers’ of short sad lives and long alive deaths, who didn't know how to do otherwise. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><strong><em>Et tu,</em> All-of-Us! Who are rehearsing the Play and making Black Holes in the Universe, while out-of-our-minds, we count our Keys, looking into the <em>void-avoiding</em> the smelly injustice being distributed!</strong><br /><br />What it worth it? The price to pay for a forbidden love?<br />Yes. Maybe. I don’t know. <br />I will need to read it again. Later.<br />Now, I need a moment. Of empty quietness.<br />To Praise. <br />To Love.<br /><br />But no words of mine would do justice to Roy’s work of art, so leave me here, hurting and loving, stabbed in the back by my own hand with the Truths of my-your-our Life, accepting a bit more of myself-your-our world, and <strong>read this real, poetic, sad, grand, too-<em>small</em>-to-be-contained Book.</strong> <br /><br />And the Kathakali dancers danced and their drummers drummed, asking their Gods to forgive them, as we also should do for the daily, unconscious murder of our Gods of Small Things.<br /><br />While it’s ten-to-two.<br /><br />Before it’s too late…<br /><br /><blockquote>———————————————————————</blockquote><br /><br />In the light of my last review of another book, where I closed its ebook covers at 20% because of typos, missing commas, too-many-grand-long-forgotten words and foreign mottos written wrongly, loose-lost opinions about historical facts, and over-the-top “<em>'pumpkin bums’</em> descriptions of nothing-happening-to-many-characters-that-had-nothing-to-do-with-any-one”, I think that to be fair to those who read my reviews, I owe an explanation to my 5 star rating for ‘The God of Small Things’.<br /><br />Roy took me through the creation and death of an ornamental garden; made me sat in a church filled with ants, a baby bat and a dead child. <br />I traveled in a bluesky Plymouth on a road full of frog stains while she uses foreign words, many half-full sentences, repeated ideas and (over-the-top, some will say) analogies. I consulted the dictionary more than a couple of times, as English is not my mother language and she uses words I was not familiar with (Probably, I would have to consult the Portuguese dictionary too).<br /><br />She made me <u>wait</u>, <strong>as a pregnant woman waits</strong>, as I read story upon story of many different characters, who <em>seemed</em> to have nothing to do with Rahel and Estha or anyone else, but were all linked somehow by society and social relationships. <br /><br />Yes, this book could have been smaller, but it could have been bigger. But if it were different, then it wouldn’t be ‘The God of Small Things’.<br /><br />I didn’t closed the book at 20% and I rated her work 5 stars.<br /><br />Why?<br />Because.<br /><br />Because there are books and books; authors and authors.<br /><br />Because I don’t care if another author has used a style before Roy used it. I don’t care if there is another author who does it better than she did it. What readers and reviewers sometimes don’t understand is that gifted authors are often gifted-avid-readers, with screaming souls begging to be set free; who drown in the works they have read and let them soak in and soothe their pains. These authors are allowed to use all the styles as their own, without being accused of <em>stealing</em> them, as I’ve seen a few reviewers raging about. And I tell you that as an avid reader with a <em>newly-freed</em> author’s soul, hoping to be one day as gifted as Roy.<br /><br />Because what I care is that, in Roy’s work, there are magical, complex, centuries of old-untold <u>
  <strong>relationships</strong>
</u> to be read about, learned and admired, in the middle of the marvel unseemly-going-nowhere descriptions of a ripple fruit bursting and an orange sun setting. <br /><br />Because Roy’s Universe is raw and rough, a few times sweet, filled with her beautiful, sharp-edged opinions - that some may think prejudiced - but are historically based and lived. She tells us an Indian story that could have been a Brazilian story. My story. Your story. <br /><br /><strong>Because what I care is that, without asking my permission, Roy took my soul and gave it back; Sadder for a moment, but more knowledgeable and fuller of passion.<br /><br />Because this is not a book for everyone, but for those who live life on its full, and are grateful for the possibility that, even being of <em>die-able</em> age, they are still alive; for those who are interested in relationships and its octopus sucking tentacles; for those who are mindful of how cruel the world can be and yet are able to see the beauty of a sunset and a strict forbidden incest love told in poetical, not-rhymed words; for those who can stand up for others in need. <br />For those who love.</strong><br /><br /><blockquote>“Because Anything can Happen to Anyone. <br />It’s Best to be Prepared.”<br />Arundhati Roy, <em>in</em> The God of Small Things</blockquote> <br /><blockquote>———————————————————————</blockquote><br /><br />P.S. 1 - I thank Steve Sckenda for having introduced me to this book with his deeper, lighter, more beautiful review. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com"></a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/290519826" target="_blank">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />P.S. 2 - If you stumble upon loose-lost reviews, overlook them and take your chance. <br />This book is like a sttuborn-child or a loved-lover, who should never be loved less, for his <em>perchance carelessness</em>, because it belongs to the Universe of Rippling Truths of Life.<!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--><!--["br"]--></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_159153787">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159153787" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2580971-umberto" class="left imgcol" title="umberto"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448362408p2/2580971.jpg" alt="umberto" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/159153787?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 11, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2580971-umberto" class="user" title="umberto">umberto</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/824360.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Paperback 9780006551096" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2580971-umberto?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer159153787">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14535952579083334769">I bought this viably readable novel last week and wondered why this one seemed familiar to me. Later I found out from the internet it was the 1997 winner of the Booker Prize, that is, its cover has kept haunting me since more than ten years ago and I wasn't aware of its formidable literary acclaim and honour. One of the reasons is that I've never read Arundhati Roy before since this is her debut worth reading critically and enjoyably.<br /><br />I kept on reading, liked it and agreed with the review that it</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14535952579083334769">I bought this viably readable novel last week and wondered why this one seemed familiar to me. Later I found out from the internet it was the 1997 winner of the Booker Prize, that is, its cover has kept haunting me since more than ten years ago and I wasn't aware of its formidable literary acclaim and honour. One of the reasons is that I've never read Arundhati Roy before since this is her debut worth reading critically and enjoyably.<br /><br />I kept on reading, liked it and agreed with the review that it's her "richly textured first book" since she's described in both tragic and comic narratives with the following styles I came across:<br /><br />1) Her ways of repetitive texts, echo-like for each one, for instance,<br /> Only that once again they broke the Love Laws. That lay down who should be loved. And how. And how much. (p. 328) vs.<br /> That it really began in the days when the Love Laws were made. The laws that lay down who should be loved, and how.<br /> And how much. (p. 33)<br />This implies one of the key themes - love sharing - of the novel.<br />2) Her shifted plot, that is, we'd find that the shameful episode at the end should've been placed somewhere before the episode of Ammu's death (p. 161), traditionally speaking.<br />3) Linguistically, she's revealed her knowledge and experience on language teaching/learning in Chapter 6 (Chochin Kangaroos) and other chapters [passim] of course. Therefore, reading them is amusing to me, for instance,<br /> Margaret Kochamma told her to Stoppit.<br /> So she Stoppited. (p. 141)<br /> 'Thang God,' Estha said.<br /> 'Thank God, Estha,' Baby Kochamma corrected him. (p. 154)<br /><br />Technically, I prefer a page or more of "Glossary" of many unfamiliar Malayalam terms/words since they should help clarify general readers (I'm sorry they're Greek to me, I could guess some meanings vaguely) to better understand and more appreciate the texts related to their particular terms/words. <br /><br />In brief, I think, the tragedy has been dictated by love presumably unfairly shared to the twins Estha and Rahel who have planned/executed their unthinkable adventure leading to Sophie Mol's drowning, such a ruthless, unimaginable fate to her. <br /><br />I look forward to reading Arundhati Roy's new novels and other writings.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14535952579083334769">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer8876823">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13841851120284920840">i know i'm alone on this one. i've never heard a single negative comment about the good of small things. plus, i love arundhati roy. i've read several of her books of essays, heard her speeches, read her occasional newspaper colums, never without utter amazement at the beautiful arrangements she composes with words. <br /><br />when i finally got around to reading the god of small things i had high hopes. that might be part of the reason why i was so disappointed with this novel. maybe i'd placed it somewhe</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13841851120284920840">i know i'm alone on this one. i've never heard a single negative comment about the good of small things. plus, i love arundhati roy. i've read several of her books of essays, heard her speeches, read her occasional newspaper colums, never without utter amazement at the beautiful arrangements she composes with words. <br /><br />when i finally got around to reading the god of small things i had high hopes. that might be part of the reason why i was so disappointed with this novel. maybe i'd placed it somewhere so high that even a booker prize couldn't reach. the bottom line was that i was bored by the plot. i don't have any greater criticism than that. i simply wasn't interested in the characters. <br /><br />roy's writing lived up to my expectations. it's lyrical, like i imagine the music coming from those paintings of angels in heaven playing the harp must sound. <br /><br />her story delves into the indian caste system and into the inner reaches of personal emotional struggles. my problem was that i had little interest in the daily affairs of her characters. </span>

        <a href="/review/show/385912?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 19, 2007</a>

      <a href="/user/show/35751-maya" class="user" title="Maya">Maya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/35751-maya?shelf=2007" class="actionLinkLite">2007</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/35751-maya?shelf=myfavorites" class="actionLinkLite">myfavorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer385912">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17858967898963140883">I literally long for Arundhati Roy to write another fiction book, even if it will only be half as beautiful as "The God of Small Things." This is one of those books that has left an indelible mark on me. I never thought one could realistically portray childhood innocence while also depicting haunting tragedy, that a book filled with so many "grown-up" issues could be narrated by a child. This isn't a book that you barrel through to find out what happens next, this is a book that you have to floa</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17858967898963140883">I literally long for Arundhati Roy to write another fiction book, even if it will only be half as beautiful as "The God of Small Things." This is one of those books that has left an indelible mark on me. I never thought one could realistically portray childhood innocence while also depicting haunting tragedy, that a book filled with so many "grown-up" issues could be narrated by a child. This isn't a book that you barrel through to find out what happens next, this is a book that you have to float through, a book whose style (some call it choppy, I find it lyrical) dictates your pace. I love love love this book.<br /><br />Also, fun trivia fact - my blog name is pulled from a phrase in this book.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17858967898963140883">...more</a>

  <a href="/user/show/1381728-maxwell" class="left imgcol" title="Maxwell"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1444015697p2/1381728.jpg" alt="Maxwell" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1009523985?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 12, 2015</a>

          marked it as dnf

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_159245116">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/159245116" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1883816-barbara" class="left imgcol" title="Barbara"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448379218p2/1883816.jpg" alt="Barbara" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/159245116?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 06, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1883816-barbara" class="user" title="Barbara">Barbara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Hardcover 9780679457312" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Barbara by: </span>
      Diane D. DeJoie
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1883816-barbara?shelf=asia" class="actionLinkLite">asia</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer159245116">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2185398374178616077">My biggest problem with this book is how to rank it. I did not enjoy a good part of it. It is bleak, dispiriting and evokes a sense of impending disaster through much of the story. The reason I am in a quandary about my sense of this novel is that I was, simply stated, delighted by Roy's unique and wonderfully creative style of writing.<br /><br />A brief synopsis of the plot will provide many examples of my ambivalence. This is the tale of a pair of near-genius, "two-egg" twin children and their eccentric</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2185398374178616077">My biggest problem with this book is how to rank it. I did not enjoy a good part of it. It is bleak, dispiriting and evokes a sense of impending disaster through much of the story. The reason I am in a quandary about my sense of this novel is that I was, simply stated, delighted by Roy's unique and wonderfully creative style of writing.<br /><br />A brief synopsis of the plot will provide many examples of my ambivalence. This is the tale of a pair of near-genius, "two-egg" twin children and their eccentric and/or maladjusted family and neighbors in India. Roy informs the reader immediately that a young visiting cousin has died. Throughout the book there is some dark mystery associated with this. <br /><br />Much of the narrative takes place around the river in their rural town. Roy related it in a fashion which meandered like the river which flows through this story. At times the language is playful, whimsical and humorous. For example: the twins were amusingly, amazingly adept at transposing words and letters in their speech, causing much consternation with the adults. Frequently Roy has fashioned words of several items linked together or completely invented them. When one of the children misbehaved, there were warnings to <em> STOPPIT </em> - <em> So SHE STOPPITED </em> ! Like the character of this river as it courses along, there were periods of darkness and upheaval which she marked by frequent capitalization of the words, also stressing their importance.<br /><br />The author has drawn an eloquent picture of the culture and the people in this area. Smells, sights and emotions were vividly portrayed. Although it is not difficult to discuss positive aspects of her writing, I would say that this was not an easy book to read. One must devote time and thought to analyzing the numerous examples of symbolism throughout. It definitely has been worth my effort. Although I have stated that this novel was dark and depressing, I will rate it 4 stars based on Roy's writing, but preferably 3.5.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2185398374178616077">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1299527070">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7367885659231233928">This was a beautiful book; both contentwise and languagewise. I do admit that it's very hard to get into because of its messy storyline and its very poetic and at times absurd writing style, but once you do you can't help but appreciate its beauty. <br />I picked up this book because it's part of BBC's Top 100 Books List that I'm currently trying to complete, and I'm very happy I did so. This book is set in India, and so it taught me about a different culture and a different way of living. It's about</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7367885659231233928">This was a beautiful book; both contentwise and languagewise. I do admit that it's very hard to get into because of its messy storyline and its very poetic and at times absurd writing style, but once you do you can't help but appreciate its beauty. <br />I picked up this book because it's part of BBC's Top 100 Books List that I'm currently trying to complete, and I'm very happy I did so. This book is set in India, and so it taught me about a different culture and a different way of living. It's about the twins Rahel and Estha who have been split at some point in life, and this story is basically a puzzle that gradually gives you the pieces to put the puzzle together. Eventually, you find out what happened to them and why their lives turned out the way they did. <br />I was a bit deceived by the fact that very little of this book takes place in America. I was under the impression that it was going to be about these two worlds. Furthermore, I do think that the highly poetical language became overwhelming at several points and kind of overshadowed the story, which I was not a fan of. But having finished the book now, I really liked it and I'm very happy that I picked it up. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7367885659231233928">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1132338361">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1132338361" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine" class="left imgcol" title="Kirstine"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1377246513p2/5172557.jpg" alt="Kirstine" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1132338361?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5172557-kirstine" class="user" title="Kirstine">Kirstine</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/117661.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Paperback 9780006550686" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=sow-2015" class="actionLinkLite">sow-2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5172557-kirstine?shelf=1-women" class="actionLinkLite">1-women</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1132338361">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4845621529164281736">“<em>it really began in the days the Love Laws were made. The laws that lay down who should be loved, and how. And how much.</em>”<br /><br /><strong>Love (Out)Laws</strong><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/367476136?book_show_action=false">Sometimes we love people we’re not supposed to love, or we love them the Wrong Way</a>. Yet ‘wrong’ loses meaning in this context. If anything was ever raised above right and wrong it’d have to be love. Love just <em>is</em>, it happens in the world, it exists beside and above everything else that goes on. You poke and prod and yell and it doesn’t move an inch. Yet we make Lo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4845621529164281736">“<em>it really began in the days the Love Laws were made. The laws that lay down who should be loved, and how. And how much.</em>”<br /><br /><strong>Love (Out)Laws</strong><br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/367476136?book_show_action=false">Sometimes we love people we’re not supposed to love, or we love them the Wrong Way</a>. Yet ‘wrong’ loses meaning in this context. If anything was ever raised above right and wrong it’d have to be love. Love just <em>is</em>, it happens in the world, it exists beside and above everything else that goes on. You poke and prod and yell and it doesn’t move an inch. Yet we make Love Laws. We govern the ungovernable. We determine what love is Right Love, when it’s right, how much is right, how to love the right way. We try, at least. <br /><br />But then you love and it’s involuntary, it creeps up on you, it’s immediate, it knocks politely or breaks the door down. You don’t get to choose, you just weather the storm, you hope you get to do it right, that you’re one of the lucky ones. You’re not. You Loved Wrong (wrong doesn’t exist but you did it wrong anyway, you’re Guilty). <br /><br />Is it worth it in the end? You don’t know. You do it anyway. <br /><br /><strong>(small things) BIG THINGS</strong><br /><br />All humans can deal with One Big Thing at a time, I think, maybe even two. More than that and we downsize some of them, they’re Medium Things, if not Small. And we like it when they stay the size they’re supposed to, because we forget that the Small Things add up, and suddenly become Big Things and there’s the trouble. You never know when it’ll happen or to whom or how many Small Things suddenly turn Big. Were they maybe always big and you were just looking the other way? Did it perhaps hide behind a person and you couldn’t see it because you were so small yourself and later, when your eyes grew with the rest of you, you’d gotten used to the shadow. You never figured it didn’t belong, until it grew so big it became a separate thing. Or maybe it’s that Small Things can be Big Things and remain the same size. <br /><br />The stone that starts the avalanche is, after all, the same size when it reaches the bottom, and still, it started an avalanche; a Big and Small Thing. This is a book about those stones, about the Small things that pile together and form a Big Thing only to fall apart again, roll to their separate places and in their place leave an Absence, a hole in the Universe. The stones: tiny, seemingly insignificant, but when they happen crucial, moments, perhaps forgotten by others, that shape every one of us. Lost loves, petty grievances, imagined insults, misunderstandings, the things that happen to us in a day, in a lifetime, and never leave. <br /><br />“<em>Perhaps it's true that things can change in a day. That a few dozen hours can affect the outcome of whole lifetimes. And that when they do, those few dozen hours, like the salvaged remains of a burned house – the charred clock, the singed photograph, the scorched furniture – must be resurrected from the ruins and examined. Preserved. Accounted for. Little events, ordinary things, smashed and reconstituted. Imbued with new meaning. Suddenly they become the bleached bones of a story.</em>”<br /><br />We incorporate them into our everyday life without issue, we reward and punish depending on our past and our hopes for the future. It works out well for everyone, most of the time, but the tension can only build for so long until it explodes. It explodes in a night of Terror, of misplaced luck and misplaced love, of History reaching out its bloody, earth-shattering hands and squeezing till you can’t breathe. <br /><br />You ask the God of Small Things for forgiveness, for the avalanche. He’s God of Most Things, you realize. But not the holes in the Universe. Those belong to the God of Loss. Sometimes they’re the same. <br /><br />(I’ve left a great many things unsaid and untouched. It may be for the better. You should experience it for yourself.)</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4845621529164281736">...more</a>

              <a href="/review/list/995100-theresa?shelf=wish-i-never-read-it" class="actionLinkLite">wish-i-never-read-it</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer17808273">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11435933093340136307">Her writing style was wonderful. I enjoyed her unique use of grammar and punctuation. The imagery was beautiful as well; but that's about where my praise ends. This particular novel falls into a category I call "Wallowing in the Most Base and Offensive Aspects of the Human Condition Without the Slightest Hint of Hope." I'm not one to shy away from uncomfortable themes and issues, but I at least like to see something constructive being done with them: a moral, an opinion, a solution, or at least</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11435933093340136307">Her writing style was wonderful. I enjoyed her unique use of grammar and punctuation. The imagery was beautiful as well; but that's about where my praise ends. This particular novel falls into a category I call "Wallowing in the Most Base and Offensive Aspects of the Human Condition Without the Slightest Hint of Hope." I'm not one to shy away from uncomfortable themes and issues, but I at least like to see something constructive being done with them: a moral, an opinion, a solution, or at least an argument of some kind. Without one of these, we're just punching ourselves in the emotional face for days (or weeks, depending on how fast you read) for no reason. And that's how I felt at the end of this book. I haven't read anything else by this author, but I hope her other works offer something more than just the regurgitation of what I see on the news.</span>

  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6968326292068597342">Best part is language. She writes prose but in a poetic language. She plays with words like little children plyas with toys. She is genius of word play. <br /><br />two examples:<br /><br /> “...the secret of the Great Stories is that they have no secrets. The Great Stories are the ones you have heard and want to hear again. The ones you can enter anywhere and inhabit comfortably. They don’t deceive you with thrills and trick endings. They don’t surprise you with the unforeseen. They are as familiar as the house you live in. Or the smell of your lover’s skin. You know how they end, yet you listen as though you don’t. In the way that although you know that one day you will die, you live as though you won’t. In the Great Stories you know who lives, who dies, who finds love, who doesn’t. And yet you want to know again.<br /><br />That is their mystery and their magic.” <br />? Arundhati Roy, The God of Small Things<br /><br /> “But what was there to say?<br /><br />Only that there were tears. Only that Quietness and Emptiness fitted together like stacked spoons. Only that there was a snuffling in the hollows at the base of a lovely throat. Only that a hard honey-colored shoulder had a semicircle of teethmarks on it. Only that they held each other close, long after it was over. Only that what they shared that night was not happiness, but hideous grief.<br /><br />Only that once again they broke the Love Laws. That lay down who should be loved. And how. And how much.” <br />? Arundhati Roy, The God of Small Things<br /><br />A very strong statement on caste system, hypocrisy of indian society, misogynistic attitude. A must read for every Indian and all others too<br /></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9777.The_God_of_Small_Things" title="Hardcover 9780679457312" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/17117710-jareed?shelf=fictional-novels" class="actionLinkLite">fictional-novels</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/17117710-jareed?shelf=historical-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer608523852">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9501070027456488470"><strong> <blockquote>“Perhaps it's true that things can change in a day. That a few dozen hours can affect the outcome of whole lifetimes. And that when they do, those few dozen hours, like the salvaged remains of a burned house---the charred clock, the singed photograph, the scorched furniture---must be resurrected from the ruins and examined. Preserved. Accounted for. Little events, ordinary things, smashed and reconstitutred. Imbued with new meaning. Suddenly they become the bleached bones of a story.”</blockquote> </strong><br /><br />Though I</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9501070027456488470"><strong>
  <blockquote>“Perhaps it's true that things can change in a day. That a few dozen hours can affect the outcome of whole lifetimes. And that when they do, those few dozen hours, like the salvaged remains of a burned house---the charred clock, the singed photograph, the scorched furniture---must be resurrected from the ruins and examined. Preserved. Accounted for. Little events, ordinary things, smashed and reconstitutred. Imbued with new meaning. Suddenly they become the bleached bones of a story.”</blockquote>
</strong><br /><br />Though I enjoyed this book thoroughly, I am perturbed by the feeling that I still read it the wrong way. I read it simultaneously with three other books and in that unprecedented predicament, I was not able to give it the undivided attention it needed and commanded. I know, I'm sorry, indescribably, incomparably and perhaps irretrievably sorry Roy. To even say that I liked this book is a gross understatement. I find that I have nothing but an abject and yet welcome lack of words for the beauty of this book, and reading you once is a mistake I would not endeavor on committing. <br /></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked &amp;&amp; exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&amp;name=' + shelf_name + '&amp;a=' + action + '&amp;authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8se6I4oAvPOWEjkUMEkhfMrQiVEjNBJvmKNuVuGAH6+XE4XweNKoCRrKTa9OKMfOgydKSGrpVcXyqmvELRXp6g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

She is intrigued by the Nawab\'s charm and aggressive courtship, and soon begins to spend most of her days in his company. But then she becomes pregnant, and unsure of the child\'s paternity, she is faced with a wrenching dilemma.           $$('div.prototip').each(function(i){i.setStyle({zIndex: 6000})});
        }
      });

      newTip['wrapper'].addClassName('prototipAllowOverflow');

        $('bookCover_299813').observe('prototip:shown', function () {
          $$('div.prototip').each(function (e) {
            if ($('bookCover_299813').hasClassName('ignored')) {
              e.setStyle({'display': 'none'});
              return;
            }
            e.setStyle({'overflow': 'visible'});
          });
        });
      $('bookCover_299813').observe('prototip:hidden', function () {
        $$('span.elementTwo').each(function (e) {
          if (e.getStyle('display') !== 'none') {
            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });
      });

//]]&gt;
</script>
</li>
</ul>

    <div class=" clearFloats bigBox" id="aboutAuthor"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/6134.Arundhati_Roy">About Arundhati Roy</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
      <a href="/author/show/6134.Arundhati_Roy"><img alt="6134" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/authors/1414594448p2/6134.jpg" class="right" /></a>
    <div class="readable">
      
<span id="freeTextContainer10502248811126192384">Arundhati Roy is an Indian writer who writes in English and an activist who focuses on issues related to social justice and economic inequality. She won the Booker Prize in 1997 for her novel, The God of Small Things, and has also written two screenplays and several collections of essays.<br /><br />For her work as an activist she received the Cultural Freedom Prize awarded by the Lannan Foundation in 2002.<br /><br /></span>

    </div>
    <a href="/author/show/6134.Arundhati_Roy" class="actionLink moreLink">More about Arundhati Roy...</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/810135-the-god-of-small-things">Quotes from The God of Small ...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">

            <span class="readable">“That's what careless words do. They make people love you a little less.”</span>
            <nobr>

              <a href="/quotes/9970-that-s-what-careless-words-do-they-make-people-love-you" class="actionLinkLite">2369 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div tyle="padding-bottom:15px" class="stacked">
            <span class="readable">“...the secret of the Great Stories is that they <em>have</em> no secrets. The Great Stories are the ones you have heard and want to hear again. The ones you can enter anywhere and inhabit comfortably. They don’t deceive you with thrills and trick endings. They don’t surprise you with the unforeseen. They are as familiar as the house you live in. Or the smell of your lover’s skin. You know how they end, yet you listen as though you don’t. In the way that although you know that one day you will die, you live as though you won’t. In the Great Stories you know who lives, who dies, who finds love, who doesn’t. And yet you want to know again.<br /><br /><em>That</em> is their mystery and their magic.”</span>
            <nobr>
              —
              <a href="/quotes/19778-the-secret-of-the-great-stories-is-that-they-have" class="actionLinkLite">758 likes</a>
